### [97] [Every Step Counts: Decoding Trajectories as Authorship Fingerprints of dLLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05148)
*Qi Li,Runpeng Yu,Haiquan Lu,Xinchao Wang*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Uses decoding trajectory structure in discrete diffusion LLMs to attribute which model produced a sequence, introducing Directed Decoding Maps and a Gaussian likelihood scorer for robust, fast attribution.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Model attribution is hard, especially distinguishing different models and even nearby checkpoints/backups. Standard per-step confidence fails in dLLMs because bidirectional decoding makes confidences redundant and obscures order/dependency structure. The authors seek a representation that preserves model-specific decoding dynamics and a principled way to score them for attribution.

Method: 1) Directed Decoding Map (DDM): extracts structural relations among decoding steps to capture bidirectional dependencies and order without being swamped by confidence redundancy. 2) Gaussian-Trajectory Attribution (GTA): for each model and each decoding position, fit a cell-wise Gaussian over DDM features; compute a sequence trajectory’s log-likelihood under each model’s distribution and attribute to the model with highest likelihood.

Result: Across varied attribution settings (different models and different checkpoints/backups), the DDM+GTA pipeline yields strong attribution performance, outperforming naive confidence-based baselines and validating the usefulness of structural decoding information.

Conclusion: Decoding trajectories of dLLMs contain distinctive, model-specific structure. By extracting it via DDM and scoring with GTA, one can reliably attribute outputs to their source dLLM across diverse scenarios, whereas per-step confidence is inadequate.

Abstract: Discrete Diffusion Large Language Models (dLLMs) have recently emerged as a
competitive paradigm for non-autoregressive language modeling. Their
distinctive decoding mechanism enables faster inference speed and strong
performance in code generation and mathematical tasks. In this work, we show
that the decoding mechanism of dLLMs not only enhances model utility but also
can be used as a powerful tool for model attribution. A key challenge in this
problem lies in the diversity of attribution scenarios, including
distinguishing between different models as well as between different
checkpoints or backups of the same model. To ensure broad applicability, we
identify two fundamental problems: what information to extract from the
decoding trajectory, and how to utilize it effectively. We first observe that
relying directly on per-step model confidence yields poor performance. This is
mainly due to the bidirectional decoding nature of dLLMs: each newly decoded
token influences the confidence of other decoded tokens, making model
confidence highly redundant and washing out structural signal regarding
decoding order or dependencies. To overcome this, we propose a novel
information extraction scheme called the Directed Decoding Map (DDM), which
captures structural relationships between decoding steps and better reveals
model-specific behaviors. Furthermore, to make full use of the extracted
structural information during attribution, we propose Gaussian-Trajectory
Attribution (GTA), where we fit a cell-wise Gaussian distribution at each
decoding position for each target model, and define the likelihood of a
trajectory as the attribution score: if a trajectory exhibits higher
log-likelihood under the distribution of a specific model, it is more likely to
have been generated by that model. Extensive experiments under different
settings validate the utility of our methods.

</details>


### [98] [Chronological Thinking in Full-Duplex Spoken Dialogue Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05150)
*Donghang Wu,Haoyang Zhang,Chen Chen,Tianyu Zhang,Fei Tian,Xuerui Yang,Gang Yu,Hexin Liu,Nana Hou,Yuchen Hu,Eng Siong Chng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They introduce Chronological Thinking, an on-the-fly, strictly causal reasoning mechanism that lets full-duplex speech dialogue models think while listening, yielding better responses without extra latency.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Full-duplex SDLMs must listen and speak simultaneously. During listening, current systems stay idle (emit silence), unlike humans who think ahead. This idle time misses an opportunity to prepare higher-quality responses and handle dynamics like barge-in more intelligently.

Method: Chronological Thinking performs incremental, strictly causal internal reasoning over the incoming audio stream. The model updates hypotheses only from past audio frames, amortizing reasoning during the listening window so that when the user stops, the agent can respond immediately with no added delay. It contrasts with Chain-of-Thought by being designed for streaming acoustic input and forbidding lookahead.

Result: Across objective metrics and human evaluations, Chronological Thinking improves response quality. It also handles conversational dynamics robustly (e.g., barge-in) and achieves competitive scores on full-duplex interaction metrics.

Conclusion: Embedding strictly causal, on-the-fly reasoning into full-duplex SDLMs leverages otherwise idle listening time to boost response quality without latency penalties, making streaming dialogue more natural and effective.

Abstract: Recent advances in spoken dialogue language models (SDLMs) reflect growing
interest in shifting from turn-based to full-duplex systems, where the models
continuously perceive user speech streams while generating responses. This
simultaneous listening and speaking design enables real-time interaction and
the agent can handle dynamic conversational behaviors like user barge-in.
However, during the listening phase, existing systems keep the agent idle by
repeatedly predicting the silence token, which departs from human behavior: we
usually engage in lightweight thinking during conversation rather than
remaining absent-minded. Inspired by this, we propose Chronological Thinking, a
on-the-fly conversational thinking mechanism that aims to improve response
quality in full-duplex SDLMs. Specifically, chronological thinking presents a
paradigm shift from conventional LLM thinking approaches, such as
Chain-of-Thought, purpose-built for streaming acoustic input. (1) Strictly
causal: the agent reasons incrementally while listening, updating internal
hypotheses only from past audio with no lookahead. (2) No additional latency:
reasoning is amortized during the listening window; once the user stops
speaking, the agent halts thinking and begins speaking without further delay.
Experiments demonstrate the effectiveness of chronological thinking through
both objective metrics and human evaluations show consistent improvements in
response quality. Furthermore, chronological thinking robustly handles
conversational dynamics and attains competitive performance on full-duplex
interaction metrics.

Motivation: LLM evaluations and real-world prompts rely on few-shot demonstrations, but the seemingly trivial decision of how to separate examples (comma, newline, semicolon, etc.) is underexplored and may bias results and model comparisons.

Method: Benchmark multiple leading LLM families (Llama, Qwen, Gemma) with identical prompts differing only in the delimiter between demonstration examples; measure accuracy (e.g., MMLU) across topics and scales; probe attention head scores to see how delimiters affect focus on key tokens; test mitigation by explicitly naming/standardizing the delimiter and identify robust delimiter choices.

Result: Performance varies by up to ±23% on MMLU solely due to delimiter choice; model rankings can be arbitrarily manipulated by changing the single separator character; brittleness persists across topics, model families, and does not diminish with scale; high-performing delimiters correlate with attention focused on key input tokens; prompting that explicitly specifies the delimiter increases robustness; practical recommendations for robust delimiters are provided.

Conclusion: Delimiter formatting is a major, overlooked confound in LLM evaluation and usage. Standardizing or declaring delimiters is necessary for fair comparisons. Abstract: Common Large Language model (LLM) evaluations rely on demonstration examples
to steer models' responses to the desired style. While the number of examples
used has been studied and standardized, the choice of how to format examples is
less investigated. In evaluation protocols and real world usage, users face the
choice how to separate in-context examples: use a comma? new line? semi-colon?

dramatically alter model response quality. Across leading model families
(Llama, Qwen, Gemma), performance on MMLU for example can vary by $\pm 23\%$
depending on the choice of delimiter. In fact, one can manipulate model
rankings to put any model in the lead by only modifying the single character
separating examples. We find LLMs' brittleness pervades topics, model families,
and doesn't improve with scale. By probing attention head scores, we find that
good-performing delimiters steer attention towards key tokens in the input.
Finally, we explore methods to improve LLMs' robustness to the choice of
delimiter. We find specifying the selected delimiter in the prompt boosts
robustness and offer practical recommendations for the best-performing
delimiters to select.

### [101] [Can AI Truly Represent Your Voice in Deliberations? A Comprehensive Study of Large-Scale Opinion Aggregation with LLMs](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05154)

### [102] [A novel hallucination classification framework](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05189)
*Maksym Zavhorodnii,Dmytro Dehtiarov,Anna Konovalenko*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often hallucinate, but scalable, reliable, and lightweight detectors are lacking. There is a need for a systematic taxonomy and dataset to study hallucination types and to enable practical detection without heavy supervision or complex models.

Method: Define a taxonomy of hallucination types and induce them through controlled prompt engineering to build a labeled hallucination dataset. Map both hallucinations and correct (veridical) responses to an embedding space, perform dimensionality reduction, apply unsupervised analysis/clustering, and compute inter-centroid distances. proposed approach is based on a systematic taxonomy and controlled reproduction
of diverse hallucination types through prompt engineering. A dedicated
hallucination dataset is subsequently mapped into a vector space using an
embedding model and analyzed with unsupervised learning techniques in a
reduced-dimensional representation of hallucinations with veridical responses.
Quantitative evaluation of inter-centroid distances reveals a consistent
correlation between the severity of informational distortion in hallucinations
and their spatial divergence from the cluster of correct outputs. These
findings provide theoretical and empirical evidence that even simple
classification algorithms can reliably distinguish hallucinations from accurate
responses within a single LLM, thereby offering a lightweight yet effective

### [103] [Let it Calm: Exploratory Annealed Decoding for Verifiable Reinforcement Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05251)
*Chenghao Yang,Lin Gui,Chenxiao Yang,Victor Veitch,Lizhu Zhang,Zhuokai Zhao*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Anneal the decoding temperature from high to low within each generation so early tokens explore and later tokens exploit, yielding better sample efficiency and stable training for RL with verifiable rewards than fixed-temperature sampling.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RLVR needs exploration that finds diverse, high-value behaviors without degrading sample quality or destabilizing training. Fixed temperatures force a trade-off: high temp hurts quality; low temp limits discovery.

Method: Exploratory Annealed Decoding (EAD): dynamically schedule temperature during decoding—start high to promote meaningful, semantic-level diversity in early tokens, then gradually lower the temperature to keep outputs close to the target policy and preserve quality, enabling stable RL training. It is a lightweight, plug-and-play replacement for fixed-temperature sampling.

Result: Across multiple RLVR algorithms and model sizes, EAD consistently outperforms fixed-temperature sampling and improves sample efficiency while maintaining training stability and output quality.

Conclusion: Aligning exploration with the sequential nature of generation—explore early, exploit late—is an effective, robust strategy for improving LLM reasoning in RLVR. EAD offers a simple, practical way to realize this alignment.

Abstract: Reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) is a powerful paradigm
for enhancing the reasoning capabilities of large language models (LLMs), yet
its success hinges on effective exploration. An ideal exploration strategy must
navigate two fundamental challenges: it must preserve sample quality while also
ensuring training stability. While standard fixed-temperature sampling is
simple, it struggles to balance these competing demands, as high temperatures
degrade sample quality and low temperatures limit discovery. In this work, we
propose a simpler and more effective strategy, Exploratory Annealed Decoding
(EAD), grounded in the insight that exploration is most impactful on early
tokens which define a sequence's semantic direction. EAD implements an
intuitive **explore-at-the-beginning, exploit-at-the-end** strategy by
annealing the sampling temperature from high to low during generation. This
dynamic schedule encourages meaningful, high-level diversity at the start, then
gradually lowers the temperature to preserve sample quality and keep the
sampling distribution close to the target policy, which is essential for stable
training. We demonstrate that EAD is a lightweight, plug-and-play method that
significantly improves sample efficiency, consistently outperforming
fixed-temperature sampling across various RLVR algorithms and model sizes. Our
work suggests that aligning exploration with the natural dynamics of sequential
generation offers a robust path to improving LLM reasoning.

### [107] [Residualized Similarity for Faithfully Explainable Authorship Verification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05362)
*Peter Zeng,Pegah Alipoormolabashi,Jihu Mun,Gourab Dey,Nikita Soni,Niranjan Balasubramanian,Owen Rambow,H. Schwartz*

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: AV decisions can have real-world consequences, demanding not only high accuracy but also explanations grounded in interpretable, text-traceable features. Current neural and LLM approaches are accurate but opaque; post-hoc explanations are not faithful to the model’s actual reasoning.

Method: Start with an interpretable AV system that computes document-to-document similarity from traceable features. Train a neural network to predict the residual (the error) between this base similarity and the true similarity, then add this residual back to obtain the final similarity score. This preserves interpretability (via the base features) while leveraging neural capacity to correct systematic errors.

Result: Across four AV datasets, RS matches state-of-the-art performance while enabling users to see how much of the decision comes from interpretable features vs. the neural residual, thus quantifying faithfulness and interpretability of the final prediction.

Conclusion: Residualized Similarity bridges the gap between accuracy and interpretability in AV by augmenting a transparent similarity model with a neural residual. It achieves SOTA-level performance and provides faithful, text-grounded explanations of predictions.

Abstract: Responsible use of Authorship Verification (AV) systems not only requires
high accuracy but also interpretable solutions. More importantly, for systems
to be used to make decisions with real-world consequences requires the model's
prediction to be explainable using interpretable features that can be traced to
the original texts. Neural methods achieve high accuracies, but their
representations lack direct interpretability. Furthermore, LLM predictions
cannot be explained faithfully -- if there is an explanation given for a
prediction, it doesn't represent the reasoning process behind the model's
prediction. In this paper, we introduce Residualized Similarity (RS), a novel
method that supplements systems using interpretable features with a neural
network to improve their performance while maintaining interpretability.
Authorship verification is fundamentally a similarity task, where the goal is
to measure how alike two documents are. The key idea is to use the neural
network to predict a similarity residual, i.e. the error in the similarity
predicted by the interpretable system. Our evaluation across four datasets
shows that not only can we match the performance of state-of-the-art authorship
verification models, but we can show how and to what degree the final
prediction is faithful and interpretable.

Compares families of methods—efficient attention variants, recurrent architectures, state space models, and hybrid designs—along axes such as asymptotic compute/memory, practical constants, training/inference characteristics, and benchmark results; identifies fundamental limitations of each class.

(2) Modern RNNs and SSMs offer strong long‑context efficiency and streaming throughput, with competitive results on some tasks, but face challenges in optimization stability, expressivity, and tooling. Conclusion: Pure‑attention Transformers remain state‑of‑the‑art overall, but their dominance is increasingly pressured. No definitive replacement yet; the near‑term path likely favors hybrids and SSM‑inspired architectures as training methods, hardware kernels, and benchmarks mature. Whether dominance is overturned will depend on resolving stability/expressivity gaps and demonstrating consistent wins across standardized long‑context benchmarks.

Abstract: Transformers have dominated sequence processing tasks for the past seven
years -- most notably language modeling. However, the inherent quadratic
complexity of their attention mechanism remains a significant bottleneck as
context length increases. This paper surveys recent efforts to overcome this
bottleneck, including advances in (sub-quadratic) attention variants, recurrent
neural networks, state space models, and hybrid architectures. We critically
analyze these approaches in terms of compute and memory complexity, benchmark
results, and fundamental limitations to assess whether the dominance of
pure-attention transformers may soon be challenged.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Prevailing belief: long-context failures stem mainly from retrieval issues. The paper asks whether perfect retrieval should make long-context performance match short-context performance, and investigates whether input length itself is a limiting factor.

Method: Systematic tests across 5 open/closed LLMs on math, QA, and coding. Controls isolate length from retrieval/distraction: (1) perfect retrieval provided, (2) irrelevant tokens replaced by whitespace, (3) irrelevant tokens masked to force attention only on relevant content, (4) evidence placed immediately before the question. Proposes a model-agnostic mitigation that converts long-context use into short-context use by prompting the model to recite the retrieved evidence before solving; evaluated on RULER with GPT-4o.

Result: Performance drops 13.9%–85% as input length increases, even within advertised context windows and under controls that eliminate retrieval failures and distraction. Recitation-before-solve yields consistent but modest gains (up to +4% on RULER for GPT-4o).

Conclusion: Long-context degradation is not solely a retrieval problem; the length of the input itself impairs LLM reasoning. Effective systems should reduce the working context (e.g., recitation/compression) and future model design/training should target length-robust reasoning, not just better retrieval.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) often fail to scale their performance on
long-context tasks performance in line with the context lengths they support.
This gap is commonly attributed to retrieval failures -- the models' inability
to identify relevant information in the long inputs. Accordingly, recent
efforts often focus on evaluating and improving LLMs' retrieval performance: if
retrieval is perfect, a model should, in principle, perform just as well on a
long input as it does on a short one -- or should it? This paper presents
findings that the answer to this question may be negative. Our systematic
experiments across 5 open- and closed-source LLMs on math, question answering,
and coding tasks reveal that, even when models can perfectly retrieve all
relevant information, their performance still degrades substantially
(13.9%--85%) as input length increases but remains well within the models'
claimed lengths. This failure occurs even when the irrelevant tokens are
replaced with minimally distracting whitespace, and, more surprisingly, when
they are all masked and the models are forced to attend only to the relevant
tokens. A similar performance drop is observed when all relevant evidence is
placed immediately before the question. Our findings reveal a
previously-unrealized limitation: the sheer length of the input alone can hurt
LLM performance, independent of retrieval quality and without any distraction.
They motivate our simple, model-agnostic mitigation strategy that transforms a
long-context task into a short-context one by prompting the model to recite the
retrieved evidence before attempting to solve the problem. On RULER, we observe
a consistent improvement of GPT-4o up to 4% on an already strong baseline.

### [113] [Self-Filtered Distillation with LLMs-generated Trust Indicators for Reliable Patent Classification](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05431)
*Yoo Yongmin,Zhang Xu,Cao Longbing*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Proposes Self-Filtered Distillation for patent classification that treats LLM-generated rationales as trust signals, using three unsupervised metrics to weight/filter training data, yielding better accuracy, stability, and interpretability on USPTO-2M.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM rationales can be noisy, logically inconsistent, and misaligned with domain labels; directly distilling them risks error propagation and unstable training. There is a need to exploit reasoning benefits without inheriting rationale noise, especially in patent classification where class definitions are specialized.

Method: Generate multiple LLM rationales per sample and compute three unsupervised trust metrics: (1) Self-Consistency across generations; (2) Class Entailment Alignment with patent class definitions; (3) LLM Agreement Scoring for rationale–label plausibility. Aggregate these into a unified trust score to weight samples and optionally filter low-trust instances, enabling reasoning-aware supervision for a student model.

Result: On the USPTO-2M benchmark, the approach outperforms label-only learning and conventional distillation in classification accuracy, training stability, and interpretability, demonstrating benefits of trust-weighted rationale use.

Conclusion: Selective, trust-scored distillation can safely leverage LLM rationales in patent analytics, mitigating noise and improving performance. Reasoning-aware trust indicators offer a robust paradigm for classification tasks with domain-specific label semantics.

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) increasingly generate natural language
rationales to enhance interpretability, but these often contain logical errors,
label mismatches, and domain-specific misalignments. Directly using such
rationales as supervision risks propagating noise and undermining training
stability. To address this challenge, we introduce Self-Filtered Distillation,
a framework specifically tailored for patent classification, which treats
LLM-generated rationales as trust signals rather than ground-truth supervision.
The framework employs selective distillation guided by three unsupervised trust
metrics: (1) Self-Consistency, which measures the stability of LLM-generated
rationales across multiple generations; (2) Class Entailment Alignment, which
assesses semantic coherence with patent-specific class definitions; and (3) LLM
Agreement Scoring, which validates rationale-label plausibility. These metrics
are integrated into a unified trust score that primarily weights training
samples while optionally filtering out extremely low-trust cases, enabling
reasoning-aware supervision. Experiments on the USPTO-2M dataset, a widely used
benchmark for patent classification, show that our method outperforms
label-based learning and conventional distillation in accuracy, stability, and
interpretability, establishing a reliable paradigm for leveraging
reasoning-aware trust indicators in patent analytics.

accelerating research in areas like RL-based model fine-tuning.

### [120] [Prototype-Based Dynamic Steering for Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05498)

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often need explicit reasoning prompts (e.g., CoT) or static steering, which is costly, brittle, or non-adaptive. There is a need for an instruction-free, test-time method that enhances reasoning adaptively per input.

Method: Prototype-Based Dynamic Steering (PDS): (1) compute activation differences between CoT and neutral prompts; (2) cluster these differences to obtain reasoning prototypes; (3) at inference, project the input’s hidden state onto the prototypes to generate an instance-specific steering vector; (4) apply this vector to steer activations—no fine-tuning or extra instructions required.

Result: On GSM8K, AQuA-RAT, and BIG-Bench tasks, PDS consistently improves accuracy without fine-tuning or prompt engineering. Improvements persist even when CoT output is suppressed for cost efficiency, implying enhanced latent reasoning rather than mere behavioral mimicry.

Conclusion: Dynamic, prototype-guided test-time steering is an effective, lightweight alternative to training-time methods for strengthening LLM reasoning, offering adaptable, instruction-free gains that generalize across tasks and remain effective without visible CoT.

Abstract: Despite impressive breadth, LLMs still rely on explicit reasoning
instructions or static, one-fits-all steering methods, leaving a gap for
adaptive, instruction-free reasoning amplification. We present Prototype-Based
Dynamic Steering (PDS), a test-time method that amplifies large language model
(LLM) reasoning without adding or altering instructions. We introduce
"reasoning prototypes" by clustering activation differences between
Chain-of-Thought (CoT) and neutral prompts. At inference, an input's hidden
state is projected onto these prototypes to form an instance-specific steering
vector. Evaluated on GSM8K, AQuA-RAT, and BIG-Bench tasks, PDS consistently
improves accuracy without fine-tuning or prompt engineering. Notably, the gains
persist even when CoT is explicitly suppressed to improve cost-efficiency,
indicating that the intervention strengthens latent reasoning processes rather
than inducing a superficial behavioral shift. These results position dynamic,
prototype-guided steering as a lightweight alternative to training-time
approaches for enhancing LLM reasoning.

### [123] [H1B-KV: Hybrid One-Bit Caches for Memory-Efficient Large Language Model Inference](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05529)
*Harshil Vejendla*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: H1B-KV is a hybrid KV-cache compression scheme for LLMs that stores keys as 1‑bit sketches and values in 4‑bit, enabling bitwise attention and slashing memory (~70×) while preserving full-precision quality after light finetuning.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLM inference with long contexts is memory-bound due to the growing KV cache. Prior approaches either quantize only part of the cache, discard tokens (losing context), or sketch only keys, leaving values uncompressed—yielding incomplete solutions. A unified, high-compression method is needed that keeps context and quality.

Method: Hybrid One-Bit KV Cache: represent each key vector with a 1-bit binary sketch to enable hardware-friendly bitwise attention; quantize value vectors to 4-bit; apply lightweight finetuning to recover any accuracy loss. Evaluated on a 7B LLM with 8k context.

Result: Achieves under 60 MB cache for 8k tokens on a 7B model (~70× reduction). After light finetuning, matches full-precision on perplexity and downstream tasks (GSM8K, MMLU, HumanEval) and surpasses leading baselines (KIVI, SparseLLM, Loki) in quality-per-byte.

Conclusion: A holistic, practical KV-cache compression approach that preserves context and model quality while drastically reducing memory, making long-context LLMs feasible in memory-constrained deployments.

Abstract: Autoregressive decoding in large language models (LLMs) requires caching a
growing list of past key-value (KV) pairs, making long-context inference a
memory-bound problem. While recent methods have explored quantizing the cache,
evicting tokens, or using binary sketches for keys (e.g., Loki), these
approaches often provide an incomplete solution by leaving one component (like
values) uncompressed or by discarding context information. This paper
introduces the Hybrid One-Bit KV Cache (H1B-KV), a comprehensive compression
scheme that radically reduces memory usage without sacrificing context. H1B-KV
represents each key vector using a 1-bit binary sketch, enabling
hardware-friendly bitwise attention, and further compresses value vectors using
4-bit quantization. This holistic, hybrid approach allows a 7-billion parameter
LLM to handle an 8k-token context with under 60 MB of cache memory - a 70x
reduction. We demonstrate that after a lightweight finetuning, H1B-KV matches
full-precision performance not only on perplexity benchmarks but also on
complex downstream tasks like mathematical reasoning (GSM8K), multi-task
understanding (MMLU), and code generation (HumanEval). Our results show H1B-KV
significantly outperforms leading quantization (KIVI), token eviction
(SparseLLM), and key-only sketching (Loki) methods in quality-per-byte,
establishing it as a robust solution for deploying LLMs in memory-constrained
environments.

### [124] [On the Role of Difficult Prompts in Self-Play Preference Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05534)
*Yao Xiao,Jung-jae Kim,Roy Ka-wei Lee,Lidong Bing*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: Prompt difficulty strongly shapes self-play preference optimization for LLMs: hard prompts (low mean RM reward over sampled responses) hurt training and don’t improve aggregate performance; pruning a calibrated fraction of them helps, and the gap shrinks with larger models.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Despite being central to self-play pipelines (LM + RM + DPO), prompts are treated uniformly. The authors want to understand how prompt difficulty affects optimization and whether training set composition can be tuned to improve alignment performance.

Method: Use the mean reward from an RM over N on-policy samples per prompt as a difficulty proxy. Compare self-play/DPO training on easy vs difficult prompts and mixtures, assess performance across model capacities, and test mitigation strategies such as selectively removing challenging prompts. They report both successful and failed attempts.

Result: Difficult prompts produce substantially worse self-play optimization than easy ones. Mixing in difficult prompts slightly degrades overall performance relative to training on easy prompts alone. As model capacity increases, the performance gap between hard and easy prompts narrows. Selective pruning of an appropriate portion of hard prompts improves overall self-play results; some alternative mitigations did not work.

Conclusion: Prompt difficulty is a critical lever in self-play preference optimization. For a given model size, careful data curation—especially dropping a calibrated share of hardest prompts—can boost outcomes. The interaction with capacity suggests curriculum or adaptive, difficulty-aware sampling/weighting strategies for future work.

Abstract: Self-play preference optimization has emerged as a prominent paradigm for
aligning large language models (LLMs). It typically involves a language model
to generate on-policy responses for prompts and a reward model (RM) to guide
the selection of chosen and rejected responses, which can be further trained
with direct preference optimization (DPO). However, the role of prompts remains
underexplored, despite being a core component in this pipeline. In this work,
we investigate how prompts of varying difficulty influence self-play preference
optimization. We first use the mean reward of $N$ sampled responses of a prompt
as a proxy for its difficulty. We find that difficult prompts exhibit
substantially inferior self-play optimization performance in comparison to easy
prompts for language models. Moreover, incorporating difficult prompts into
training fails to enhance overall performance and, in fact, leads to slight
degradation compared to training on easy prompts alone. We also observe that
the performance gap between difficult and easy prompts closes as the model
capacity increases, suggesting that difficulty interacts with the model
capacity. Building on these findings, we explore strategies to mitigate the
negative effect of difficult prompts on final performance. We demonstrate that
selectively removing an appropriate portion of challenging prompts enhances
overall self-play performance, while also reporting failed attempts and lessons
learned.

### [125] [Activation-Informed Pareto-Guided Low-Rank Compression for Efficient LLM/VLM](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05544)

  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs and VLMs are costly to deploy due to memory and compute demands. state-of-the-art performance, but they impose significant memory and computing
challenges in deployment. We present a novel low-rank compression framework to
address this challenge. First, we upper bound the change of network loss via
layer-wise activation-based compression errors, filling a theoretical gap in

planning in long-horizon tasks. In this paper, we introduce a plan-and-execute
framework and propose EAGLET, an efficient and effective planner training
method to enhance the executor agent's planning abilities without human effort.
Specifically, we train a plug-and-play global planner through a two-step
process: we first synthesize high-quality plans from an advanced LLM using our
proposed homologous consensus filtering strategy, and apply fine-tuning as a
cold start. Moreover, we further improve the planner with a rule-based
reinforcement learning stage using a novel executor capability gain reward,
ensuring it can handle task instructions of varying difficulty. Experiments on
three long-horizon agent tasks show that executor agents equipped with our
planner outperform existing methods, achieving new state-of-the-art
performance. Meanwhile, EAGLET reduces training costs by 8x compared to
RL-based baselines, and it does not require manual effort or extra training
data, offering an efficient and effective solution.

### [134] [COSPADI: Compressing LLMs via Calibration-Guided Sparse Dictionary Learning](https://arxiv.org/abs/2509.22075)
*Dmitriy Shopkhoev,Denis Makhov,Magauiya Zhussip,Ammar Ali,Stamatios Lefkimmiatis*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: CoSpaDi is a training-free LLM compression method that replaces low-rank with structured sparse dictionary factorization optimized on a small calibration set to match layer activations, yielding 20–50% compression with better accuracy/perplexity than state-of-the-art data-aware low-rank approaches and compatibility with quantization.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Low-rank post-training compression constrains all columns to a shared subspace, often causing accuracy loss. The goal is to increase expressiveness and preserve functional fidelity without fine-tuning while remaining efficient for inference.

Method: Factorize each weight matrix W ≈ D·C where D is a dense dictionary and C is column-sparse. This enables a union-of-subspaces representation (different columns use different subsets of atoms). Optimize D and C using a small calibration dataset to minimize mismatch between outputs of original and compressed projection layers (functional reconstruction) rather than pure weight error. The structured sparsity supports efficient sparse–dense matmul and combines with post-training quantization.

Result: Across Llama and Qwen models, under per-layer and per-group schemes at 20–50% compression, CoSpaDi consistently outperforms state-of-the-art data-aware low-rank baselines in accuracy and perplexity, with additional memory/latency benefits and no fine-tuning required.

Conclusion: Structured sparse dictionary learning is a strong alternative to low-rank for post-training LLM compression, offering higher expressiveness, better fidelity under reasonable compression ratios, and compatibility with quantization for efficient deployment.

Abstract: Post-training compression of large language models (LLMs) largely relies on
low-rank weight approximation, which represents each column of a weight matrix
in a shared low-dimensional subspace. While this is a computationally efficient
strategy, the imposed structural constraint is rigid and can lead to a
noticeable model accuracy drop. In this work, we propose CoSpaDi (Compression
via Sparse Dictionary Learning), a novel training-free compression framework
that replaces low-rank decomposition with a more flexible structured sparse
factorization in which each weight matrix is represented with a dense
dictionary and a column-sparse coefficient matrix. This formulation enables a
union-of-subspaces representation: different columns of the original weight
matrix are approximated in distinct subspaces spanned by adaptively selected
dictionary atoms, offering greater expressiveness than a single invariant
basis. Crucially, CoSpaDi leverages a small calibration dataset to optimize the
factorization such that the output activations of compressed projection layers
closely match those of the original ones, thereby minimizing functional
reconstruction error rather than mere weight approximation. This data-aware
strategy preserves better model fidelity without any fine-tuning under
reasonable compression ratios. Moreover, the resulting structured sparsity
allows efficient sparse-dense matrix multiplication and is compatible with
post-training quantization for further memory and latency gains. We evaluate
CoSpaDi across multiple Llama and Qwen models under per-layer and per-group
settings at 20-50\% compression ratios, demonstrating consistent superiority
over state-of-the-art data-aware low-rank methods both in accuracy and
perplexity. Our results establish structured sparse dictionary learning as a
powerful alternative to conventional low-rank approaches for efficient LLM
deployment.

</details>


### [135] [Diversity Is All You Need for Contrastive Learning: Spectral Bounds on Gradient Magnitudes](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05767)
*Peter Ochieng*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They derive tight, non-asymptotic spectral bounds linking the InfoNCE gradient norm to temperature and the batch spectrum, recover the 1/τ^2 law, and turn this into spectrum-aware batching and in-batch whitening that speed training at equal accuracy and reduce gradient variance.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Contrastive learning with InfoNCE can exhibit gradient anisotropy and instability that depend on temperature and batch composition. The authors aim to predict and control gradient magnitude/variance to make training more stable and sample-efficient, and to turn spectral insights into practical data/batch selection strategies.

Method: (1) Derive non-asymptotic spectral bands for the squared InfoNCE gradient norm as a function of feature alignment, temperature τ, and the batch covariance spectrum. (2) Use effective rank Reff as a proxy for anisotropy. (3) Design spectrum-aware batch selection policies, including a fast greedy builder, and apply in-batch whitening to promote isotropy. (4) Validate bounds and methods on synthetic data, ImageNet (ImageNet-100), and CIFAR-10.

Result: The theory recovers the 1/τ^2 scaling and closely predicts batch-mean gradient norms on synthetic data and ImageNet. Spectrum-aware batching (Greedy-64) cuts time-to-67.5% top-1 on ImageNet-100 by 15% vs random (24% vs Pool–P3) at equal accuracy, with similar gains on CIFAR-10. In-batch whitening reduces 50-step gradient variance by 1.37×, matching their theoretical upper bound.

Conclusion: Batch-spectrum-aware training and whitening guided by spectral analysis of InfoNCE gradients improve training efficiency without sacrificing accuracy. Effective rank is a useful anisotropy proxy, and the derived bounds are practically tight, offering a principled route to lower-variance, faster contrastive learning.

Abstract: We derive non-asymptotic spectral bands that bound the squared InfoNCE
gradient norm via alignment, temperature, and batch spectrum, recovering the
\(1/\tau^{2}\) law and closely tracking batch-mean gradients on synthetic data
and ImageNet. Using effective rank \(R_{\mathrm{eff}}\) as an anisotropy proxy,
we design spectrum-aware batch selection, including a fast greedy builder. On
ImageNet-100, Greedy-64 cuts time-to-67.5\% top-1 by 15\% vs.\ random (24\%
vs.\ Pool--P3) at equal accuracy; CIFAR-10 shows similar gains. In-batch
whitening promotes isotropy and reduces 50-step gradient variance by
\(1.37\times\), matching our theoretical upper bound.

*Jianbin Shen,Christy Jie Liang,Junyu Xuan*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They propose two training methods to boost informativeness in abstractive summarization: an optimal-transport-based informative attention and a named-entity joint-entropy reduction, yielding better ROUGE on CNN/DailyMail and competitive XSum, with human studies confirming higher informativeness.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Abstractive summarization must distill long, voluminous text into concise, coherent, and informative summaries. Despite progress, models often miss salient information. The paper targets improving informativeness—capturing focal content and salient entities more reliably.

Method: (1) Optimal transport-based informative attention to better align the model’s focus with informative segments of reference summaries during learning. (2) Accumulative joint entropy reduction on named entities to encourage selection and stabilization of salient entities, increasing information salience. These are integrated into training to guide attention and content selection.

Result: On CNN/DailyMail, the approach surpasses prior ROUGE results; on XSum, it remains competitive. Human evaluation indicates improved informativeness over a strong baseline. Additional analyses provide plausible explanations for these outcomes.

Conclusion: Guiding attention via optimal transport and constraining entity uncertainty improves summary informativeness without sacrificing general performance, offering a principled way to make abstractive summaries more informative; analyses suggest why, and the approach generalizes reasonably across datasets.

Abstract: Abstractive text summarization is integral to the Big Data era, which demands
advanced methods to turn voluminous and often long text data into concise but
coherent and informative summaries for efficient human consumption. Despite
significant progress, there is still room for improvement in various aspects.
One such aspect is to improve informativeness. Hence, this paper proposes a
novel learning approach consisting of two methods: an optimal transport-based
informative attention method to improve learning focal information in reference
summaries and an accumulative joint entropy reduction method on named entities
to enhance informative salience. Experiment results show that our approach
achieves better ROUGE scores compared to prior work on CNN/Daily Mail while
having competitive results on XSum. Human evaluation of informativeness also
demonstrates the better performance of our approach over a strong baseline.
Further analysis gives insight into the plausible reasons underlying the
evaluation results.

### [137] [Mixture of Neuron Experts](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05781)
*Runxi Cheng,Yuchen Guan,Yucheng Ding,Qingguo Hu,Yongxian Wei,Chun Yuan,Yelong Shen,Weizhu Chen,Yeyun Gong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MoE experts exhibit strong within-expert activation sparsity; leveraging this with a simple per-expert top‑k neuron selection (MoNE) preserves accuracy while halving activated parameters and adding negligible overhead.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Although MoE routes tokens to a few experts to save compute, many parameters within the selected experts contribute little at inference. The authors ask whether the actually activated parameters are still overly redundant—and if so, can this sparsity be exploited to improve efficiency and utilization without extra routing complexity?

Method: 1) Empirical sparsification: within each expert, rank parameters by gate-projection activation magnitudes and progressively prune the activated subset; visualize neuron activations by decomposing experts into neuron-level units. 2) Observation: most neuron activations are near zero. 3) Proposal: Mixture of Neuron Experts (MoNE), which performs simple top‑k selection of high-activation neurons within each expert during pretraining/inference, requiring no extra routing parameters or inter-expert communication and incurring negligible latency.

Result: Pruning up to 60% of the activated parameters per expert leads to negligible performance loss; large drops occur only after >90% pruning. MoNE matches traditional MoE while activating only 50% of MoE-layer parameters and consistently outperforms traditional MoE when matched on the number of activated parameters.

Conclusion: Within-expert activations are highly sparse and can be exploited with lightweight neuron-level selection. MoNE offers a practical, low-overhead path to better parameter utilization and faster inference in MoE-like models without complicating routing.

Abstract: In this work, we first explore whether the parameters activated by the MoE
layer remain highly sparse at inference. We perform a sparsification study on
several representative MoE models. For each expert, we rank parameters by the
magnitude of their activations from the gate projection and progressively prune
the activated subset. Pruning up to 60% of parameters within that subset causes
only negligible task-performance degradation; substantial drops occur only
after more than 90% are removed. We further decompose experts into
neuron-granular MoE and visualize their activation values, finding that most
neuron activations are near zero. This observation motivates us to select only
high-activation neuron experts during pretraining. Based on this insight, we
propose Mixture of Neuron Experts (MoNE). MoNE achieves neuron-granular expert
selection by only applying a simple top-k selection within each expert, incurs
negligible latency, and requires no additional routing parameters or
inter-expert communication. Extensive experiments demonstrate that MoNE matches
traditional MoE performance while activating only 50% of the MoE-layer
parameters, and it consistently outperforms traditional MoE when compared at
equal numbers of activated parameters. These results suggest that MoNE is a
practical approach to improving parameter utilization and inference efficiency
in MoE-like models.

</details>


### [138] [Data-efficient Targeted Token-level Preference Optimization for LLM-based Text-to-Speech](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05799)
*Rikuto Kotoge,Yuichi Sasaki*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: TKTO is a pair-free, token-level preference optimization method for TTS that automatically derives fine-grained rewards, yielding large gains in pronunciation accuracy and error reduction (notably for Japanese).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Paired desirable/undesirable utterances are scarce, and utterance-level optimization cannot precisely correct pronunciation; a data-efficient, fine-grained alignment method is needed to better match human preferences.

Method: Introduce TKTO, which eliminates the need for paired utterances and token-level annotations by directly optimizing token-level units using automatically inferred fine-grained alignment signals (reward shaping) from preference feedback in language model-based TTS.

Result: On Japanese TTS, TKTO improves accuracy by 39%, reduces character error rate by 54%, and assigns 12.8× stronger reward to targeted tokens, indicating focused correction at problematic units.

Conclusion: Token-level, pair-free preference optimization enables more data-efficient and precise alignment of TTS outputs with human preferences, improving robustness and naturalness—especially pronunciation—without costly paired data or annotations.

Abstract: Aligning text-to-speech (TTS) system outputs with human feedback through
preference optimization has been shown to effectively improve the robustness
and naturalness of language model-based TTS models. Current approaches
primarily require paired desirable and undesirable samples at the utterance
level. However, such pairs are often limited in TTS output data, and
utterance-level formulation prevents fine-grained token-level optimization
needed for accurate pronunciation alignment. In this study, we propose TKTO
that eliminates the need for paired data, enabling a more data-efficient
training paradigm, and directly targets token-level units, automatically
providing fine-grained alignment signals without token-level annotations. TKTO
improves the challenging Japanese TTS accuracy by 39% and reduces CER by 54%,
automatically assigning 12.8 times stronger reward to targeted tokens.

### [139] [EEPO: Exploration-Enhanced Policy Optimization via Sample-Then-Forget](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05837)
*Liang Chen,Xueting Han,Qizhou Wang,Bo Han,Jing Bai,Hinrich Schutze,Kam-Fai Wong*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EEPO is a two-stage RL framework for LLMs that temporarily “unlearns” sampled responses between rollout stages to break mode lock-in, boosting exploration and outperforming GRPO on reasoning tasks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: RL with verifiable rewards for LLMs often collapses into over-exploitation: policies keep sampling and rewarding dominant modes, reducing entropy and exploratory capacity, which limits performance gains.

Method: Exploration-Enhanced Policy Optimization (EEPO): conduct rollouts in two stages. After generating half the trajectories in stage one, apply a lightweight adaptive unlearning step that suppresses those sampled responses. This sample-then-forget step discourages repeating the same modes, forcing stage-two rollouts to explore new output regions before policy optimization.

Result: Across five reasoning benchmarks, EEPO beats GRPO with average relative gains of 24.3% (Qwen2.5-3B), 33.0% (Llama3.2-3B-Instruct), and 10.4% (Qwen3-8B-Base).

Conclusion: Temporarily unlearning sampled outputs disrupts self-reinforcing exploitation loops, increases exploration during rollouts, and yields consistent performance improvements in RLVR for LLMs.

Abstract: Balancing exploration and exploitation remains a central challenge in
reinforcement learning with verifiable rewards (RLVR) for large language models
(LLMs). Current RLVR methods often overemphasize exploitation, leading to
entropy collapse, diminished exploratory capacity, and ultimately limited
performance gains. Although techniques that increase policy stochasticity can
promote exploration, they frequently fail to escape dominant behavioral modes.
This creates a self-reinforcing loop-repeatedly sampling and rewarding dominant
modes-that further erodes exploration. We introduce Exploration-Enhanced Policy
Optimization (EEPO), a framework that promotes exploration via two-stage
rollouts with adaptive unlearning. In the first stage, the model generates half
of the trajectories; it then undergoes a lightweight unlearning step to
temporarily suppress these sampled responses, forcing the second stage to
explore different regions of the output space. This sample-then-forget
mechanism disrupts the self-reinforcing loop and promotes wider exploration
during rollouts. Across five reasoning benchmarks, EEPO outperforms GRPO,
achieving average relative gains of 24.3% on Qwen2.5-3B, 33.0% on
Llama3.2-3B-Instruct, and 10.4% on Qwen3-8B-Base.

</details>

Abstract: Long-context models (LCMs) have demonstrated great potential in processing
long sequences, facilitating many real-world applications. The success of LCMs
can be attributed to their ability to locate implicit critical information
within the context for further prediction. However, recent research reveals
that LCMs are often susceptible to contextual noise, i.e., irrelevant tokens,
that can mislead model attention. In this paper, we conduct a fine-grained
analysis of the context noise and propose an effective metric, the Integrated
Gradient (IG) score, to detect and quantify the noise information within the
context. Our findings reveal that even simple mitigation of detected context
noise can substantially boost the model's attention on critical tokens and
benefit subsequent predictions. Building on this insight, we propose Context
Denoising Training (CDT), a straightforward yet effective training strategy
that improves attention on critical tokens while reinforcing their influence on
model predictions. Extensive experiments across four tasks, under both context

  <summary>Details</summary>

Method: Benchmark evaluation varying four factors: (1) harmful content type (explicit vs implicit), (2) position in the prompt (beginning/middle/end), (3) prevalence proportion (0.01–0.50 of tokens), and (4) overall context length (600–6000 tokens). Tested across multiple harm categories (toxic, offensive, hate) and three model families (LLaMA‑3, Qwen‑2.5, Mistral). Measured detection performance (e.g., precision/recall).

Result: Across models, consistent trends emerge: peak performance at moderate harmful prevalence (~0.25); performance drops when harmful content is very sparse or dominates the prompt; recall degrades as context length increases; sentences at the beginning are detected more reliably than middle/end; explicit harms are more consistently recognized than implicit ones.

Conclusion: LLMs’ safety filters in long contexts are sensitive to signal salience and position, and suffer from context dilution and implicitness. extended context, from document processing to retrieval-augmented generation.
While their long-context capabilities are well studied for reasoning and
retrieval, little is known about their behavior in safety-critical scenarios.
We evaluate LLMs' sensitivity to harmful content under extended context,
varying type (explicit vs. implicit), position (beginning, middle, end),
prevalence (0.01-0.50 of the prompt), and context length (600-6000 tokens).
Across harmful content categories such as toxic, offensive, and hate speech,
with LLaMA-3, Qwen-2.5, and Mistral, we observe similar patterns: performance
peaks at moderate harmful prevalence (0.25) but declines when content is very
sparse or dominant; recall decreases with increasing context length; harmful
sentences at the beginning are generally detected more reliably; and explicit
content is more consistently recognized than implicit. These findings provide
the first systematic view of how LLMs prioritize and calibrate harmful content
in long contexts, highlighting both their emerging strengths and the challenges
that remain for safety-critical use.

### [148] [EvalMORAAL: Interpretable Chain-of-Thought and LLM-as-Judge Evaluation for Moral Alignment in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.05942)
*Hadi Mohammadi,Anastasia Giachanou,Ayoub Bagheri*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: EvalMORAAL is a transparent chain-of-thought evaluation framework that scores moral alignment in 20 LLMs using both log-probabilities and direct ratings, plus a model-as-judge peer review. Models correlate highly with global survey data overall (≈0.90 on WVS), but show a sizable regional gap (Western r=0.82 vs non-Western r=0.61). Peer agreement predicts survey alignment, enabling automated quality checks.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Assessing LLMs’ moral alignment needs culturally aware, fair, and transparent evaluation. Prior work lacks standardized scoring across heterogeneous models, robust reasoning protocols, and scalable quality control; it rarely quantifies regional disparities. The authors aim to provide a reproducible framework that can compare models fairly and detect culturally grounded bias.

Method: Evaluate 20 LLMs on World Values Survey (55 countries, 19 topics) and PEW Global Attitudes Survey (39 countries, 8 topics). Use two scoring paradigms (log-probabilities and direct ratings) under a structured chain-of-thought with self-consistency checks. Add a model-as-judge peer review to flag conflicts via a data-driven threshold. Quantify alignment via Pearson correlations between model outputs and survey responses; analyze regional differences and relate peer agreement to survey alignment.

Result: Top models achieve high alignment with survey responses (WVS r≈0.90). There is a consistent regional disparity: Western regions average r=0.82 vs non-Western r=0.61 (absolute gap 0.21). The peer-review process flags 348 conflicts, and peer agreement correlates with survey alignment (WVS r=0.74; PEW r=0.39; both p<.001).

Conclusion: EvalMORAAL advances culture-aware evaluation with transparent CoT, dual scoring for fair comparison, and automated peer review. Despite strong overall alignment, substantial regional bias persists, highlighting open challenges for reliable cross-regional deployment and the need for improved methods to capture diverse cultural values.

Abstract: We present EvalMORAAL, a transparent chain-of-thought (CoT) framework that
uses two scoring methods (log-probabilities and direct ratings) plus a
model-as-judge peer review to evaluate moral alignment in 20 large language
models. We assess models on the World Values Survey (55 countries, 19 topics)
and the PEW Global Attitudes Survey (39 countries, 8 topics). With EvalMORAAL,
top models align closely with survey responses (Pearson's r approximately 0.90
on WVS). Yet we find a clear regional difference: Western regions average
r=0.82 while non-Western regions average r=0.61 (a 0.21 absolute gap),
indicating consistent regional bias. Our framework adds three parts: (1) two
scoring methods for all models to enable fair comparison, (2) a structured
chain-of-thought protocol with self-consistency checks, and (3) a
model-as-judge peer review that flags 348 conflicts using a data-driven
threshold. Peer agreement relates to survey alignment (WVS r=0.74, PEW r=0.39,
both p<.001), supporting automated quality checks. These results show real
progress toward culture-aware AI while highlighting open challenges for use
across regions.

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>

Abstract: Large language models (LLMs) are increasingly deployed on complex reasoning

locate the specific attention heads of the final Transformer layer: these
attention heads have opposite activation patterns for simple and difficult
problems, thus achieving perception of difficulty. Our ablation experiments
prove the accuracy of the location. Crucially, our experiments provide
practical support for using LLMs as automatic difficulty annotators,
potentially substantially reducing reliance on costly human labeling in
benchmark construction and curriculum learning. We also uncover that there is a
significant difference in entropy and difficulty perception at the token level.

Method: Construct a benchmark (LexiCon) by taking standard, unconstrained planning environments and automatically imposing temporal state constraints, translating each constrained problem into natural language prompts for LLMs. The framework is extensible: new environment generators can be added and constraints are automatically derived, enabling scaling difficulty over time.

Result: Across experiments with strong reasoning models (e.g., GPT-5, o3, R1), performance degrades as the level of constrainedness increases, indicating difficulty for LLMs in adhering to temporal and safety constraints during planning.

environments and impose temporal constraints on the states. These constrained
problems are then translated into natural language and given to an LLM to
solve. A key feature of LexiCon is its extensibility. That is, the set of
supported environments can be extended with new (unconstrained) environment
generators, for which temporal constraints are constructed automatically. This
renders LexiCon future-proof: the hardness of the generated planning problems
can be increased as the planning capabilities of LLMs improve. Our experiments
reveal that the performance of state-of-the-art LLMs, including reasoning
models like GPT-5, o3, and R1, deteriorates as the degree of constrainedness of

Method: Construct an 8-permutation paradigm of refined parasitic gap (PG) stimuli and evaluate GPT-2 (as in prior work) using Wilcox-style direct minimal pair comparisons to compute the “wh-effect.” Analyze performance systematically across four key PG conditions to test filler–gap licensing in complex PG environments.

### [152] [MASA: Rethinking the Representational Bottleneck in LoRA with Multi-A Shared Adaptation](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.06005)
*Qin Dong,Yuntian Tang,Heming Jia,Yunhang Shen,Bohan Jia,Wenxuan Huang,Lianyue Zhang,Jiao Xie,Shaohui Lin*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: MASA replaces LoRA’s single down-projection with a small ensemble of shared “A” experts and a layer-specific “B,” mitigating LoRA’s representational bottleneck and yielding modest but consistent gains (e.g., +1.08 points on MMLU) at comparable parameter cost (~0.52%).


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LoRA relies on one down-projection matrix (A) as the sole feature extractor per layer, which can be too limited to capture diverse task signals. The authors aim to enrich feature adaptation without sacrificing parameter efficiency to improve downstream task performance and generalization.

Method: Introduce Multi-A Shared Adaptation (MASA): multiple specialized A experts per layer, with asymmetric sharing of these A experts across layers to limit parameter growth; each layer retains a single B matrix that integrates the expert outputs. This multi-A, single-B design expands representational capacity while maintaining PEFT efficiency.

Result: Across multi-domain generalization, single-domain specialization, and multi-task reasoning, MASA outperforms standard LoRA under similar parameter budgets. On MMLU, MASA reaches 59.62% average accuracy, improving by 1.08 absolute points (1.84% relative) with ~0.52% learnable parameters.

Conclusion: Expanding the down-projection side via shared multi-A experts alleviates LoRA’s bottleneck and provides better adaptation at nearly the same parameter cost, suggesting that richer feature extraction—rather than larger B or more parameters overall—is a key lever for stronger PEFT.

Abstract: Low-Rank Adaptation (LoRA) has emerged as a dominant method in
Parameter-Efficient Fine-Tuning (PEFT) for large language models, which
augments the transformer layer with one down-projection $A$ and one
up-projection $B$. However, LoRA's reliance on a single down-projection matrix
($A$) creates a representational bottleneck, as this solitary feature extractor
is inherently insufficient for capturing the diverse signals required by
complex tasks. This motivates our architectural shift to focus on enriching the
feature adaptation to improve the downstream task adaptation ability. We
propose MASA (Multi-$A$ Shared Adaptation), an architecture that implements a
multi-$A$, single-$B$ structure where the multi-$A$ expert ensemble is
asymmetrically shared across layers to ensure parameter efficiency. In MASA,
these specialized experts capture diverse features, which are then integrated
by a single, layer-specific $B$-matrix. The effectiveness and versatility of
our method are validated through a comprehensive suite of experiments spanning
multi-domain generalization, single-domain specialization, and multi-task
reasoning. For example, on the MMLU benchmark, MASA achieves an average
accuracy of 59.62%, outperforming the standard LoRA by 1.08 points (a relative
improvement of 1.84%) with comparable learnable parameters of 0.52%.

</details>

### [155] [ASPO: Asymmetric Importance Sampling Policy Optimization](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.06062)
*Jiakang Wang,Runze Liu,Lei Lin,Wenping Hu,Xiu Li,Fuzheng Zhang,Guorui Zhou,Kun Gai*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: They identify a mismatch in token-level importance sampling (IS) used by outcome-supervised RL for LLMs that over-weights already-likely tokens and suppresses low-probability ones; they fix it with an asymmetric IS scheme (ASPO) plus soft dual clipping, yielding more stable training and better coding/math performance than GRPO baselines.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Outcome-supervised RL methods for LLM post-training commonly use token-level clipping with IS ratios. The current practice mismatches IS ratios for positive-advantage tokens, causing unbalanced weighting that accelerates premature convergence by amplifying high-probability tokens and dampening learning on low-probability, potentially useful tokens.

Method: Propose Asymmetric Importance Sampling Policy Optimization (ASPO): flip the IS ratios for positive-advantage tokens so their update direction matches the learning dynamics of negative tokens; add a soft dual-clipping mechanism to tame extreme updates while preserving gradient flow. The approach is a simple drop-in change to existing OSRL/GRPO-style training.

Result: Across coding and mathematical reasoning benchmarks, ASPO reduces premature convergence, improves training stability, and improves final performance compared to strong GRPO-based baselines. Code and models are released at https://github.com/wizard-III/Archer2.0.

Conclusion: Correcting token-level IS is critical in OSRL for LLMs. An asymmetric IS correction with soft dual clipping better balances token updates, leading to more stable optimization and superior performance over GRPO-style methods.

Abstract: Recent Large Language Model (LLM) post-training methods rely on token-level
clipping mechanisms during Reinforcement Learning (RL). However, we identify a
fundamental flaw in this Outcome-Supervised RL (OSRL) paradigm: the Importance
Sampling (IS) ratios of positive-advantage tokens are mismatched, leading to
unbalanced token weighting for positive and negative tokens. This mismatch
suppresses the update of low-probability tokens while over-amplifying already
high-probability ones. To address this, we propose Asymmetric Importance
Sampling Policy Optimization (ASPO), which uses a simple yet effective strategy
that flips the IS ratios of positive-advantage tokens, aligning their update
direction with the learning dynamics of negative ones. AIS further incorporates
a soft dual-clipping mechanism to stabilize extreme updates while maintaining
gradient flow. Comprehensive experiments on coding and mathematical reasoning
benchmarks demonstrate that ASPO significantly mitigates premature convergence,
improves training stability, and enhances final performance over strong
GRPO-based baselines. Our analysis provides new insights into the role of
token-level weighting in OSRL and highlights the critical importance of
correcting IS in LLM RL. The code and models of ASPO are available at
https://github.com/wizard-III/Archer2.0.

</details>


### [156] [Spectrum Tuning: Post-Training for Distributional Coverage and In-Context Steerability](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.06084)

<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: Instruction-tuned LMs often struggle on tasks with many valid outputs, showing reduced flexibility, output diversity, and poor alignment to user-specified distributions. The field lacks clear desiderata and comprehensive benchmarks to measure and improve this kind of conditional distributional modeling.

Method: (1) Formalize three desiderata: in-context steerability, valid output space coverage, and distributional alignment; distinguish elicitive ICL vs steerability ICL. (2) Empirically evaluate effects of standard post-training across three model families. (3) Compile Spectrum Suite (>40 sources, >90 tasks) to stress-test distributional control across human preference and numerical distributions, etc. (4) Propose Spectrum Tuning, a post-training approach leveraging Spectrum Suite to improve steerability and coverage.

Result: Standard post-training helps elicit latent knowledge but degrades in-context steerability and distributional properties. Spectrum Tuning reverses much of this degradation, outperforming both pretrained and instruction-tuned baselines by improving steerability, broadening valid output coverage, and better aligning output distributions on held-out datasets.

Conclusion: Effective post-training must balance instruction-following with distributional flexibility. Spectrum Suite provides a comprehensive evaluation resource, and Spectrum Tuning offers a practical remedy that enhances steerability and alignment without sacrificing capability elicitation.

Abstract: Language model post-training has enhanced instruction-following and
performance on many downstream tasks, but also comes with an often-overlooked
cost on tasks with many possible valid answers. We characterize three
desiderata for conditional distributional modeling: in-context steerability,
valid output space coverage, and distributional alignment, and document across
three model families how current post-training can reduce these properties. In
particular, we disambiguate between two kinds of in-context learning: ICL for
eliciting existing underlying knowledge or capabilities, and in-context
steerability, where a model must use in-context information to override its
priors and steer to a novel data generating distribution. To better evaluate
and improve these desiderata, we introduce Spectrum Suite, a large-scale
resource compiled from >40 data sources and spanning >90 tasks requiring models
to steer to and match diverse distributions ranging from varied human
preferences to numerical distributions and more. We find that while current
post-training techniques help elicit underlying capabilities and knowledge,
they hurt models' ability to flexibly steer in-context. To mitigate these
issues, we propose Spectrum Tuning, a post-training method using Spectrum Suite
to improve steerability and distributional coverage. We find that Spectrum
Tuning often improves over pretrained models and their instruction-tuned
counterparts, enhancing steerability, spanning more of the output space, and
improving distributional alignment on held-out datasets.

Result: Observed a valley-shaped scaling curve: as distillation data increases, performance initially drops, then rises faster than log-linear. Abstract: Distilling the thinking traces of a Large Language Model (LLM) with reasoning
capabilities into a smaller model has been proven effective. Yet, there is a
scarcity of work done on how model performances scale with the quantity of
distillation data. In this work, we study the scaling trend of distilling
competitive coding skills on two small non-reasoning LLMs. We validate the
hypothesis that there is a $\textit{valley of code reasoning}$: downstream
performance on competitive coding first drops as data quantity increases, then
it steadily increases in a sharper-than-log-linear fashion. Having identified
the trend, we further fine-tune the models at two different distillation stages
on the same data to ground conclusions on their respective learning phases. We

benefit significantly from easier coding questions than from harder ones. We

### [158] [Distributional Semantics Tracing: A Framework for Explaining Hallucinations in Large Language Models](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.06107)
*Gagan Bhatia,Somayajulu G Sripada,Kevin Allan,Jacobo Azcona*

Main category: cs.CL

TL;DR: DST traces internal semantics in LLMs, revealing a “commitment layer” where hallucinations become inevitable and a dual-pathway conflict (fast associative vs slow contextual). Contextual-pathway coherence strongly inversely correlates with hallucination rates (ρ = -0.863), offering a mechanistic account of hallucinations.


<details>
  <summary>Details</summary>
Motivation: LLMs often generate plausible but false statements (hallucinations). The authors seek an intrinsic, architectural explanation and a reliable way to trace where and how internal semantic failures arise so they can be predicted and diagnosed.

Method: They introduce Distributional Semantics Tracing (DST), a unified framework that integrates established interpretability techniques to construct a causal map of a model’s reasoning under distributional semantics. Using DST, they locate the layer where internal representations diverge from factuality (the “commitment layer”), decompose computation into an associative (System 1-like) vs contextual (System 2-like) pathway, and quantify the coherence of the contextual pathway.

Result: DST identifies a commitment layer at which hallucinations become inevitable; reveals a conflict between associative and contextual pathways, producing failure modes such as Reasoning Shortcut Hijacks; and shows a strong negative correlation (ρ = -0.863) between contextual-pathway coherence and hallucination rates.

Conclusion: Hallucinations in Transformers are predictable outcomes of weak contextual semantics and competition with fast associative processes. DST offers a mechanistic explanation of how, when, and why hallucinations occur, enabling principled diagnosis and potential mitigation strategies.

Abstract: Large Language Models (LLMs) are prone to hallucination, the generation of
plausible yet factually incorrect statements. This work investigates the
intrinsic, architectural origins of this failure mode through three primary
contributions.First, to enable the reliable tracing of internal semantic
failures, we propose \textbf{Distributional Semantics Tracing (DST)}, a unified
framework that integrates established interpretability techniques to produce a
causal map of a model's reasoning, treating meaning as a function of context
(distributional semantics). Second, we pinpoint the model's layer at which a
hallucination becomes inevitable, identifying a specific \textbf{commitment
layer} where a model's internal representations irreversibly diverge from
factuality. Third, we identify the underlying mechanism for these failures. We
observe a conflict between distinct computational pathways, which we interpret
using the lens of dual-process theory: a fast, heuristic \textbf{associative
pathway} (akin to System 1) and a slow, deliberate \textbf{contextual pathway}
(akin to System 2), leading to predictable failure modes such as
\textit{Reasoning Shortcut Hijacks}. Our framework's ability to quantify the
coherence of the contextual pathway reveals a strong negative correlation
($\rho = -0.863$) with hallucination rates, implying that these failures are
predictable consequences of internal semantic weakness. The result is a
mechanistic account of how, when, and why hallucinations occur within the
Transformer architecture.
